<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Great Pyramid" redirects here. </th></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Great Pyramid of Giza</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> is the largest <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">Egyptian pyramid</a> and the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Fourth Dynasty of Egypt">Fourth Dynasty</a> pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Khufu" title="Khufu">Khufu</a>. Built in the early 26th century BC during a period of around 27 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETallet2017_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETallet2017-4">[3]</a></sup> the pyramid is the oldest of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">Seven Wonders of the Ancient World</a>, and the only one to remain largely intact. As part of the <a href="/wiki/Giza_pyramid_complex" title="Giza pyramid complex">Giza pyramid complex</a>, it borders present-day <a href="/wiki/Giza" title="Giza">Giza</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_Cairo" title="Greater Cairo">Greater Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. 
</p><p>Initially standing at 146.6 metres (481 feet), the Great Pyramid was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_and_structures#History" title="List of tallest buildings and structures">tallest man-made structure</a> in the world for more than 3,800 years. Over time, most of the smooth white limestone casing was removed, which lowered the pyramid's height to the present 138.5 metres (454.4 ft). What is seen today is the underlying core structure. The base was measured to be about 230.3 metres (755.6 ft) square, giving a volume of roughly 2.6 million cubic metres (92 million cubic feet), which includes an internal hillock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehnerHawass2017143,_530–531_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehnerHawass2017143,_530–531-5">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dimensions of the pyramid were 280 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_cubit" title="Royal cubit">royal cubits</a> (146.7 m; 481.4 ft) high, a base length of 440 cubits (230.6 m; 756.4 ft), with a <a href="/wiki/Seked" title="Seked">seked</a> of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac" role="math">5<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="tion"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></span> palms (a slope of 51°50'40").
</p><p>The Great Pyramid was built by quarrying an estimated 2.3 million large blocks weighing 6 million tonnes in total. The majority of stones are not uniform in size or shape and are only roughly dressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fabric_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fabric-6">[5]</a></sup> The outside layers were bound together by mortar. Primarily local limestone from the <a href="/wiki/Giza_Plateau" title="Giza Plateau">Giza Plateau</a> was used. Other blocks were imported by boat down the Nile: White limestone from <a href="/wiki/Tura,_Egypt" title="Tura, Egypt">Tura</a> for the casing, and granite blocks from <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>, weighing up to 80 tonnes, for the King's Chamber structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997207_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997207-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are three known chambers inside the Great Pyramid. The lowest was cut into the bedrock, upon which the pyramid was built, but remained unfinished. The so-called<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomer20078_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomer20078-8">[7]</a></sup> Queen's Chamber and King's Chamber, that contains a granite sarcophagus, are higher up, within the pyramid structure. Khufu's <a href="/wiki/Vizier_(Ancient_Egypt)" title="Vizier (Ancient Egypt)">vizier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemiunu" title="Hemiunu">Hemiunu</a> (also called Hemon), is believed by some to be the architect of the Great Pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw200389_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw200389-9">[8]</a></sup> Many varying scientific and alternative hypotheses attempt to explain the exact construction techniques.
</p><p>The funerary complex around the pyramid consisted of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mortuary_temples" title="Mortuary temples">mortuary temples</a> connected by a causeway (one close to the pyramid and one near the Nile), tombs for the immediate family and court of Khufu, including three smaller pyramids for Khufu's wives, an even smaller "satellite pyramid" and five buried <a href="/wiki/Solar_barque" title="Solar barque">solar barges</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attribution_to_Khufu">Attribution to Khufu</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khufu_seal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="922" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Khufu_seal.jpg/220px-Khufu_seal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Khufu_seal.jpg/330px-Khufu_seal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Khufu_seal.jpg/440px-Khufu_seal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khufu_seal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Clay seal bearing the name of Khufu from the Great Pyramid on display at the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> museum</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="180" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg/220px-Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg/330px-Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Goyon-inscriptions-divers23.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Khufu's cartouche found inscribed on a backing stone of the pyramid.</div></div></div>
<p>Historically the Great Pyramid had been attributed to Khufu based on the words of authors of classical antiquity, first and foremost Herodotus and Diodorus Siculus. However, during the middle ages a number of other people were credited with the construction of the pyramid as well, for example <a href="/wiki/Joseph_(Genesis)" title="Joseph (Genesis)">Joseph</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nimrod" title="Nimrod">Nimrod</a> or king <a href="/wiki/Surid_Ibn_Salhouk" title="Surid Ibn Salhouk">Saurid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreaves1752612_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreaves1752612-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1837 four additional Relieving Chambers were found above the King's Chamber after tunneling to them. The chambers, previously inaccessible, were covered in hieroglyphs of red paint. The workers who were building the pyramid had marked the blocks with the names of their gangs, which included the pharaoh's name (e.g.: “The gang, The <a href="/wiki/Hedjet" title="Hedjet">white crown</a> of Khnum-Khufu is powerful”). The names of Khufu were spelled out on the walls over a dozen times. Another of these graffiti was found by <a href="/wiki/Georges_Goyon" title="Georges Goyon">Goyon</a> on an exterior block of the 4th layer of the pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> The inscriptions are comparable to those found at other sites of Khufu, such as the alabaster quarry at Hatnub<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> or the harbor at <a href="/wiki/Wadi_al-Jarf" title="Wadi al-Jarf">Wadi al-Jarf</a>, and are present in pyramids of other pharaohs as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reisner1931_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reisner1931-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the 20th century the cemeteries next to the pyramid were excavated. Family members and high officials of Khufu were buried in the <a href="/wiki/Giza_East_Field" title="Giza East Field">East Field</a> south of the causeway, and the <a href="/wiki/Giza_West_Field" title="Giza West Field">West Field</a>. Most notably the wives, children and grandchildren of Khufu, <a href="/wiki/Hemiunu" title="Hemiunu">Hemiunu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ankhhaf" title="Ankhhaf">Ankhaf</a> and (the funerary cache of) <a href="/wiki/Hetepheres_I" title="Hetepheres I">Hetepheres I</a>, mother of Khufu. As Hassan puts it: "From the early dynastic times, it was always the custom for the relatives, friends and courtiers to be buried in the vicinity of the king they had served during life. This was quite in accordance with the Egyptian idea of the Hereafter."
</p><p>The cemeteries were actively expanded until the 6th dynasty and used less frequently afterwards. The earliest pharaonic name of seal impressions is that of Khufu, the latest of Pepi II. Worker graffiti was written on some of the stones of the tombs as well; for instance, "Mddw" (Horus name of Khufu) on the <a href="/wiki/Mastaba" title="Mastaba">mastaba</a> of Chufunacht, probably a grandson of Khufu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some inscriptions in the chapels of the mastabas (like the pyramid, their burial chambers were usually bare of inscriptions) mention Khufu or his pyramid. For instance, an inscription of Mersyankh III states that "Her mother [is the] daughter of the King of Upper and Lower Egypt Khufu." Most often these references are part of a title, for example, Snnw-ka, "Chief of the Settlement and Overseer of the Pyramid City of Akhet-Khufu" or Merib, "Priest of Khufu".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Several tomb owners have a king's name as part of their own name (e.g. Chufudjedef, Chufuseneb, Merichufu). The earliest pharaoh alluded to in that manner at Giza is Snefru (Khufu's father).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHassan1960_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHassan1960-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReisner1942_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReisner1942-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1936 Hassan uncovered a <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">stela</a> of <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_II" title="Amenhotep II">Amenhotep II</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Great_Sphinx_of_Giza" title="Great Sphinx of Giza">Great Sphinx of Giza</a> which implies the two larger pyramids were still attributed to Khufu and Khafre in the New Kingdom. It reads: "He yoked the horses in Memphis, when he was still young, and stopped at the Sanctuary of Hor-em-akhet (the Sphinx). He spent a time there in going round it, looking at the beauty of the Sanctuary of Khufu and Khafra the revered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHassan19603_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHassan19603-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954 two boat pits, one containing the <a href="/wiki/Khufu_ship" title="Khufu ship">Khufu ship</a>, were discovered buried at the south foot of the pyramid. The cartouche of <a href="/wiki/Djedefre" title="Djedefre">Djedefre</a> was found on many of the blocks that covered the boat pits. As the successor and eldest son he would have presumably been responsible for the burial of Khufu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> The second boat pit was examined in 1987; excavation work started in 2010. Graffiti on the stones included 4 instances of the name "Khufu", 11 instances of "Djedefre", a year (in reign, season, month and day), measurements of the stone, various signs and marks, and a reference line used in construction, all done in red or black ink.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>During excavations in 2013 the <a href="/wiki/Diary_of_Merer" title="Diary of Merer">Diary of Merer</a> was found at <a href="/wiki/Wadi_al-Jarf" title="Wadi al-Jarf">Wadi al-Jarf</a>. It documents the transportation of white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Limestone_block" title="Limestone block">limestone blocks</a> from Tura to the Great Pyramid, which is mentioned by its original name Akhet Khufu (with a pyramid <a href="/wiki/Determinative" title="Determinative">determinative</a>) dozens of times. It details that the stones were accepted at She Akhet-Khufu ("the pool of the pyramid Horizon of Khufu") and Ro-She Khufu (“the entrance to the pool of Khufu”) which were under supervision of <a href="/wiki/Ankhhaf" title="Ankhhaf">Ankhhaf</a>, half brother and vizier of Khufu, as well as owner of the largest mastaba of the Giza East Field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETallet2017_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETallet2017-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>O'Mara (1997)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Mara1997_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Mara1997-30">[29]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2700 BC
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Beckarath (1997)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2554 BC
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Arnold (1999)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnold1999_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnold1999-32">[31]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2551 BC
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Spence (2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spence2000_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spence2000-33">[32]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2480 BC
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Shaw (2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw2000_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw2000-34">[33]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2589 BC
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Hornung (2006)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hornung2006_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hornung2006-35">[34]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2509 BC
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Ramsey et al. (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramsey2010_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsey2010-36">[35]</a></sup>
</td>
<td align="right">2613–2577 BC
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Great Pyramid has been determined to be about 4600 years old by two principal approaches: indirectly, through its attribution to Khufu and his chronological age, based on archaeological and textual evidence; and directly, via radiocarbon dating of organic material found in the pyramid and included in its mortar.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_chronology">Historical chronology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_chronology" title="Egyptian chronology">Egyptian chronology</a></div>
<p>In the past the Great Pyramid was dated by its attribution to Khufu alone, putting the construction of the Great Pyramid within his reign. Hence dating the pyramid was a matter of dating Khufu and the 4th dynasty. The relative sequence and synchronicity of events stands at the focal point of this method.
</p><p>Absolute calendar dates are derived from an interlocked network of evidence, the backbone of which are the lines of succession known from ancient king lists and other texts. The reign lengths from Khufu to known points in the earlier past are summated, bolstered with genealogical data, astronomical observations, and other sources. As such, the historical chronology of Egypt is primarily a political chronology, thus independent from other types of archaeological evidence like stratigraphies, material culture, or radiocarbon dating.
</p><p>The majority of recent chronological estimates date Khufu and his pyramid roughly between 2700 and 2500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Höflmayer2016_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Höflmayer2016-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiocarbon_dating">Radiocarbon dating</span></h3>
<p>Mortar was used generously in the Great Pyramid's construction. In the mixing process ashes from fires were added to the mortar, organic material that could be extracted and <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dated</a>. A total of 46 samples of the mortar were taken in 1984 and 1995, making sure they were clearly inherent to the original structure and could not have been incorporated at a later date. The results were calibrated to 2871–2604 BC. The <a href="/wiki/Old_wood" title="Old wood">old wood problem</a> is thought to be mainly responsible for the 100–300 year offset, since the age of the organic material was determined, not when it was last used. A reanalysis of the data gave a completion date for the pyramid between 2620 and 2484 BC, based on the younger samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1872 <a href="/wiki/Waynman_Dixon" title="Waynman Dixon">Waynman Dixon</a> opened the lower pair of "Air-Shafts", previously closed at both ends, by chiseling holes into the walls of the Queen's Chamber. One of the objects found within was a cedar plank, which came into possession of James Grant, a friend of Dixon. After inheritance it was donated to the Museum of Aberdeen in 1946, however it had broken into pieces and was filed incorrectly. Lost in the vast museum collection, it was only rediscovered in 2020, when it was radiocarbon dated to 3341–3094 BC. Being over 500 years older than Khufu's chronological age, Abeer Eladany suggests that the wood originated from the center of a long-lived tree or had been recycled for many years prior to being deposited in the pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_dating_Khufu_and_the_Great_Pyramid">History of dating Khufu and the Great Pyramid</span></h3>
<p>Circa 450 BC Herodotus attributed the Great Pyramid to Cheops (Hellenization of Khufu), yet erroneously placed his reign following the Ramesside period. <a href="/wiki/Manetho" title="Manetho">Manetho</a>, around 200 years later, composed an extensive list of Egyptian kings which he divided into dynasties, assigning Khufu to the 4th. However, after phonetic changes in the Egyptian language and consequently the Greek translation, "Cheops" had transformed into "Souphis" (and similar versions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Greaves" title="John Greaves">Greaves</a>, in 1646, reported the great difficulty of ascertaining a date for the pyramid's construction based on the lacking and conflicting historic sources. Because of the aforementioned differences in spelling, he didn't recognize Khufu on Manetho's king list (as transcribed by <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Julius_Africanus" title="Sextus Julius Africanus">Africanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> hence he relied on Herodotus' incorrect account. Summating the duration of lines of succession, Greaves concluded the year 1266 BC to be the beginning of Khufu's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreaves1752615–623_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreaves1752615–623-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two centuries later, some of the gaps and uncertainties in Manetho's chronology had been cleared by discoveries such as the King Lists of <a href="/wiki/Turin_King_List" title="Turin King List">Turin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abydos_King_List" title="Abydos King List">Abydos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karnak_King_List" title="Karnak King List">Karnak</a>. The names of Khufu found within the Great Pyramid's Relieving Chambers in 1837 helped to make clear that Cheops and Souphis are, in fact, one and the same. Thus the Great Pyramid was recognized to have been built in the 4th dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lepsius1849_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lepsius1849-25">[24]</a></sup> The dating among Egyptologists still varied by multiple centuries (around 4000–2000 BC), depending on methodology, preconceived religious notions (such as the biblical deluge) and which source they thought was more credible.
</p><p>Estimates significantly narrowed in the 20th century, most being within 250 years of each other, around the middle of the third millennium BC. The newly developed radiocarbon dating method confirmed that the historic chronology was approximately correct. It is, however, still not a fully appreciated method due to larger margins or error, calibration uncertainties and the problem of inbuilt age (time between growth and final usage) in plant material, including wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Höflmayer2016_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Höflmayer2016-37">[36]</a></sup> Furthermore, astronomical alignments have been suggested to coincide with the time of construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Mara1997_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-O'Mara1997-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spence2000_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spence2000-33">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egyptian chronology continues to be refined and data from multiple disciplines have started to be factored in, such as luminescence dating, radiocarbon dating, and dendrochronology. For instance, Ramsey et al. included over 200 radiocarbon samples in their model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ramsey2010_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ramsey2010-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiographical_record">Historiographical record</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Herodotus">Herodotus</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2650" data-file-width="2130" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg/200px-Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg/300px-Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg/400px-Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_Massimo_Inv124478.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> was one of the first major authors to discuss the Great Pyramid.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">ancient Greek</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, writing in the 5th century BC, is one of the first major authors to mention the pyramid. In the second book of his work <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">The Histories</a></i>, he discusses the history of Egypt and the Great Pyramid. This report was created more than 2000 years after the structure was built, meaning that Herodotus obtained his knowledge mainly from a variety of indirect sources, including officials and priests of low rank, local Egyptians, Greek immigrants, and Herodotus's own interpreters. Accordingly, his explanations present themselves as a mixture of comprehensible descriptions, personal descriptions, erroneous reports, and fantastical legends; as such, many of the speculative errors and confusions about the monument can be traced back to Herodotus and his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase2004a125_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase2004a125-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards1986990–991_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards1986990–991-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Herodotus writes that the Great Pyramid was built by Khufu (Hellenized as Cheops) who, he erroneously relays, ruled after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramesside_Period" title="Ramesside Period">Ramesside Period</a> (Dynasties XIX and XX).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiodorus_Siculus1933216_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiodorus_Siculus1933216-46">[45]</a></sup> Khufu was a tyrannical king, Herodotus claims, which may explain the Greek's view that such buildings can only come about through cruel exploitation of the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase2004a125_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase2004a125-44">[43]</a></sup> Herodotus further states that gangs of 100,000 labourers worked on the building in three-month shifts, taking 20 years to build. In the first ten years a wide causeway was erected, which, according to Herodotus, was almost as impressive as the construction of the pyramids themselves. It measured nearly 1 kilometre (0.62 mi) long and 20 yards (18.3 m) wide, and elevated to a height of 16 yards (14.6 m), consisting of stone polished and carved with figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h124_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-h124-47">[46]</a></sup> In addition, underground chambers were made on the hill whereon the pyramids stand. These were intended to be burial places for Khufu himself and were encompassed with water by a channel brought in from the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h124_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-h124-47">[46]</a></sup> Herodotus later states that at the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Khafre" title="Pyramid of Khafre">Pyramid of Khafre</a> (located beside the Great Pyramid) the Nile flows through a built passage to an island in which Khufu is buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Zahi_Hawass" title="Zahi Hawass">Hawass</a> interprets this to be a reference to the "<a href="/wiki/Giza_pyramid_complex#Shafts" title="Giza pyramid complex">Osiris Shaft</a>" which is located at the causeway of Khafre, south of the Great Pyramid.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Herodotus also described an inscription on the outside of the pyramid which, according to his translators, indicated the amount of radishes, garlic and onions that the workers would have eaten while working on the pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> This could be a note of restoration work that <a href="/wiki/Khaemweset" title="Khaemweset">Khaemweset</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Rameses II</a>, had carried out. Apparently, Herodotus’ companions and interpreters could not read the hieroglyphs or deliberately gave him false information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase2004a127_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase2004a127-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diodorus_Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</span></h4>
<p>Between 60–56 BC, the ancient Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> visited Egypt and later dedicated the first book of his <i><a href="/wiki/Bibliotheca_historica" title="Bibliotheca historica">Bibliotheca historica</a></i> to the land, its history, and its monuments, including the Great Pyramid. Diodorus's work was inspired by historians of the past, but he also distanced himself from Herodotus, who Diodorus claims tells marvelous tales and myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Diodorus presumably drew his knowledge from the lost work of <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Abdera" title="Hecataeus of Abdera">Hecataeus of Abdera</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShawBloxam20211157_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShawBloxam20211157-54">[53]</a></sup> and like Herodotus, he also places the builder of the pyramid, "Chemmis,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds63_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ds63-55">[54]</a></sup> after Ramses III.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDiodorus_Siculus1933216_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiodorus_Siculus1933216-46">[45]</a></sup> According to his report, neither Chemmis (<a href="/wiki/Khufu" title="Khufu">Khufu</a>) nor Cephren (<a href="/wiki/Khafre" title="Khafre">Khafre</a>) were buried in their pyramids, but rather in secret places, for fear that the people ostensibly forced to build the structures would seek out the bodies for revenge;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds64_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ds64-56">[55]</a></sup> with this assertion, Diodorus strengthened the connection between pyramid building and slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurton1972189_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurton1972189-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Diodorus, the cladding of the pyramid was still in excellent condition at the time, whereas the uppermost part of the pyramid was formed by a platform 6 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_cubit" title="Royal cubit">cubits</a> (3.1 m; 10.3 ft) high. About the construction of the pyramid he notes that it was built with the help of ramps since no lifting tools had yet been invented. Nothing was left of the ramps, as they were removed after the pyramids were completed. He estimated the number of workers necessary to erect the Great Pyramid at 360,000 and the construction time at 20 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds63_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-ds63-55">[54]</a></sup> Similar to Herodotus, Diodorus also claims that the side of the pyramid is inscribed with writing that "[set] forth [the price of] vegetables and purgatives for the workmen there were paid out over sixteen hundred talents."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds64_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-ds64-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Strabo">Strabo</span></h4>
<p>The Greek geographer, philosopher, and historian <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> visited Egypt around 25 BC, shortly after <a href="/wiki/War_of_Actium" title="War of Actium">Egypt was annexed by the Romans</a>. In his work <i><a href="/wiki/Geographica" title="Geographica">Geographica</a></i>, he argues that the pyramids were the burial place of kings, but he does not mention which king was buried in the structure. Strabo also mentions: "At a moderate height in one of the sides is a stone, which may be taken out; when that is removed, there is an oblique passage to the tomb."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-s1734_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-s1734-58">[57]</a></sup> This statement has generated much speculation, as it suggests that the pyramid could be entered at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883217_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883217-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pliny_the_Elder">Pliny the Elder</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1591" data-file-width="1291" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg/220px-Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg/330px-Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg/440px-Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grande_Illustrazione_del_Lombardo_Veneto_Vol_3_Plinio_Secondo_300dpi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> argues that "bridges" were used to transport stones to the top of the Great Pyramid.</div></div></div>
<p>The Roman writer <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, writing in the first century AD, argued that the Great Pyramid had been raised, either "to prevent the lower classes from remaining unoccupied", or as a measure to prevent the pharaoh's riches from falling into the hands of his rivals or successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pliny_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pliny-60">[59]</a></sup> Pliny does not speculate as to the pharaoh in question, explicitly noting that "accident [has] consigned to oblivion the names of those who erected such stupendous memorials of their vanity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pe3617_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-pe3617-61">[60]</a></sup> In pondering how the stones could be transported to such a vast height he gives two explanations: That either vast mounds of <a href="/wiki/Niter" title="Niter">nitre</a> and salt were heaped up against the pyramid which were then melted away with water redirected from the river. Or, that "bridges" were constructed, their bricks afterwards distributed for erecting houses of private individuals, arguing that the level of the river is too low for canals to ever bring water up to the pyramid. Pliny also recounts how "in the interior of the largest Pyramid there is a well, eighty-six <a href="/wiki/Cubit#Ancient_Egyptian_royal_cubit" title="Cubit">cubits</a> [45.1 m; 147.8 ft] deep, which communicates with the river, it is thought". Further, he describes a method discovered by <a href="/wiki/Thales_of_Miletus" title="Thales of Miletus">Thales of Miletus</a> for ascertaining the pyramid's height by measuring its shadow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pe3617_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-pe3617-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_antiquity_and_the_Middle_Ages">Late antiquity and the Middle Ages</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph%27s_Granaries" title="Joseph's Granaries">Joseph's Granaries</a></div>
<p>During late antiquity, a misinterpretation of the pyramids as "Joseph's granary" began to gain in popularity. The first textual evidence of this connection is found in the travel narratives of the female Christian pilgrim <a href="/wiki/Egeria_(pilgrim)" title="Egeria (pilgrim)">Egeria</a>, who records that on her visit between 381–384 AD, "in the twelve-mile stretch between Memphis and Babylonia [= Old Cairo] are many pyramids, which Joseph made in order to store corn."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> Ten years later the usage is confirmed in the anonymous travelogue of seven monks that set out from Jerusalem to visit the famous ascetics in Egypt, wherein they report that they "saw Joseph's granaries, where he stored grain in biblical times."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> This late 4th century usage is further confirmed in the geographical treatise <i>Cosmographia</i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Honorius" title="Julius Honorius">Julius Honorius</a> around 376 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> which explains that the Pyramids were called the "granaries of Joseph" (<i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">horrea Ioseph</i></span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> This reference from Julius is important, as it indicates that the identification was starting to spread out from pilgrim's travelogues. In 530 AD, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stephanos_of_Byzantium" title="Stephanos of Byzantium">Stephanos of Byzantium</a> added more to this idea when he wrote in his <i>Ethnica</i> that the word "pyramid" was connected to the Greek word πυρός (<i>puros</i>), meaning wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchironi2009119–120_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchironi2009119–120-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="642" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png/220px-Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png/330px-Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png/440px-Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al-Ma%CA%BEm%C5%ABn.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a> (786–833 CE) is said to have tunneled into the side of the Great Pyramid.</div></div></div>
<p>In the seventh century AD, the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">conquered Egypt</a>, ending several centuries of Romano-Byzantine rule. A few centuries later, in 820 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a> (786–833) is said to have tunneled into the side of the structure and discovered the ascending passage and its connecting chambers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooperson2010165_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooperson2010165-67">[66]</a></sup> Around this time a Coptic legend gained popularity that claimed the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_flood_narrative" title="Genesis flood narrative">antediluvian</a> king <a href="/wiki/Surid_Ibn_Salhouk" title="Surid Ibn Salhouk">Surid Ibn Salhouk</a> had built the Pyramid. One legend in particular relates how, three hundred years prior to the Great Flood, Surid had a terrifying dream of the world's end, and so he ordered the construction of the pyramids so that they might house all the knowledge of Egypt and survive into the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEColavito201551–55_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColavito201551–55-68">[67]</a></sup> The most notable account of this legend was given by <a href="/wiki/Al-Masudi" title="Al-Masudi">Al-Masudi</a> (896–956) in his <i>Akbar al-zaman</i>, alongside imaginative tales about the pyramid, such as the story of a man who fell three hours down the pyramid's well and the tale of an expedition that discovered bizarre finds in the structure's inner chambers. <i>Al-zaman</i> also contains a report of Al-Ma'mun's entering the pyramid and discovering a vessel containing a thousand coins, which just so happened to account for the cost of opening the pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b321–330_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b321–330-69">[68]</a></sup> (Some speculate that this story is true, but that the coins were planted by Al-Ma'mun to appease his workers, who were likely frustrated that they had found no treasure.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETompkins197117_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETompkins197117-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 987 AD, the Arab bibliographer <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Nadim" title="Ibn al-Nadim">Ibn al-Nadim</a> relates a fantastical tale in his <i><a href="/wiki/Al-Fihrist" title="Al-Fihrist">Al-Fihrist</a></i> about a man who journeyed into the main chamber of a pyramid, which <a href="/wiki/Bayard_Dodge" title="Bayard Dodge">Bayard Dodge</a> argues is the Great Pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIbn_al-Nadim1970846_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIbn_al-Nadim1970846-71">[70]</a></sup> According to al-Nadim, the person in question saw a statue of a man holding a tablet and a woman holding a mirror. Supposedly, between the statues was a "stone vessel [with] a gold cover." Inside the vessel was "something like <a href="/wiki/Pitch_(resin)" title="Pitch (resin)">pitch</a>," and when the explorer reached into the vessel "a gold receptacle happened to be inside." The receptacle, when taken from the vessel, was filled with "fresh blood," which quickly dried up. Ibn al-Nadim's work also claims that the bodies of a man and woman were discovered inside the Pyramid in the "best possible state of preservation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIbn_al-Nadim1970846–847_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIbn_al-Nadim1970846–847-72">[71]</a></sup> The author al-Kaisi, in his work the <i>Tohfat Alalbab,</i> retells the story of Al-Ma'mun's entry but with the additional discovery of "an image of a man in green stone", which when opened revealed a body dressed in jewel-encrusted gold armor. Al-Kaisi claims to have seen the case from which the body was taken, and asserts that it was located at the king's palace in Cairo. He also writes that he himself entered into the pyramid and discovered myriad preserved bodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b333–334_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b333–334-73">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Arab polymath <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Latif_al-Baghdadi" title="Abd al-Latif al-Baghdadi">Abd al-Latif al-Baghdadi</a> (1163–1231) studied the pyramid with great care, and in his <i>Account of Egypt</i>, he praises them as works of engineering genius. In addition to measuring the structure (alongside the other pyramids at Giza), al-Baghdadi also writes that the structures were surely tombs, although he thought the Great Pyramid was used for the burial of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agathodaimon" title="Agathodaimon">Agathodaimon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a>. Al-Baghdadi ponders whether the pyramid pre-dated the Great flood as described in Genesis, and even briefly entertained the idea that it was a pre-Adamic construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiggs201737–38_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiggs201737–38-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEl_Daly200548–49_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEl_Daly200548–49-75">[74]</a></sup> A few centuries later, the Islamic historian <a href="/wiki/Al-Maqrizi" title="Al-Maqrizi">Al-Maqrizi</a> (1364–1442) compiled lore about the Great Pyramid in his <i>Al-Khitat</i>. In addition to reasserting that Al-Ma'mun breached the structure in 820 AD, Al-Maqrizi's work also discusses the sarcophagus in the coffin chambers, explicitly noting that the pyramid was a grave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the close of the Middle Ages, the Great Pyramid had gained a reputation as a haunted structure. Others feared entering because it was home to animals like bats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETompkins197121–27_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETompkins197121–27-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction">Construction</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation_of_the_site">Preparation of the site</span></h3>
<p>A hillock forms the base on which the pyramids stands. It was cut back into steps and only a strip around the perimeter was leveled,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner2017214_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner2017214-78">[77]</a></sup> which has been measured to be horizontal and flat to within 21 millimetres (0.8 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997109_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997109-79">[78]</a></sup> The bedrock reaches a height of almost 6 metres (20 ft) above the pyramid base at the location of the Grotto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Along the sides of the base platform a series of holes are cut in the bedrock. Lehner hypothesizes that they held wooden posts used for alignment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> Edwards, among others, suggested the usage of water for evening the base, although it is unclear how practical and workable such a system would be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner2017214_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner2017214-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials">Materials</span></h3>
<p>The Great Pyramid consists of an estimated 2.3 million blocks. Approximately 5.5 million tonnes of limestone, 8,000 tonnes of granite, and 500,000 tonnes of mortar were used in the construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomer2007157_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomer2007157-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the blocks were <a href="/wiki/Quarry" title="Quarry">quarried</a> at Giza just south of the pyramid, an area now known as the <a href="/wiki/Central_Field,_Giza" title="Central Field, Giza">Central Field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The white limestone used for the casing originated from <a href="/wiki/Tura,_Egypt" title="Tura, Egypt">Tura</a> (10 km (6.2 mi) south of Giza) and was transported by boat down the Nile. In 2013, rolls of papyrus called the <a href="/wiki/Diary_of_Merer" title="Diary of Merer">Diary of Merer</a> were discovered, written by a supervisor of the deliveries of <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> and other construction materials from <a href="/wiki/Tura,_Egypt" title="Tura, Egypt">Tura</a> to Giza in the last known year of Khufu's reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> stones in the pyramid were transported from <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>, more than 900 km (560 mi) away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997207_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997207-7">[6]</a></sup> The largest, weighing 25 to 80 tonnes, form the roofs of the "King's chamber" and the "relieving chambers" above it. Ancient Egyptians cut stone into rough blocks by hammering grooves into natural stone faces, inserting wooden wedges, then soaking these with water. As the water was absorbed, the wedges expanded, breaking off workable chunks. Once the blocks were cut, they were carried by boat either up or down the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> to the pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997202_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997202-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Workforce">Workforce</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> believed that <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Egypt" title="Slavery in ancient Egypt">slave labour</a> was used, but modern discoveries made at nearby workers' camps associated with construction at Giza suggest that it was built instead by thousands of conscript laborers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner199739,_224_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner199739,_224-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Worker graffiti found at Giza suggest haulers were divided into <i>zau</i> (singular <i>za</i>), groups of 40 men, consisting of four sub-units that each had an "Overseer of Ten".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETallet2017_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETallet2017-4">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>As to the question of how over two million blocks could have been cut within Khufu's lifetime, stonemason Franck Burgos conducted an <a href="/wiki/Experimental_archaeology" title="Experimental archaeology">archaeological experiment</a> based on an abandoned quarry of Khufu discovered in 2017. Within it, an almost completed block and the tools used for cutting it had been uncovered: hardened <a href="/wiki/Arsenical_bronze" title="Arsenical bronze">arsenic copper</a> chisels, wooden mallets, ropes and stone tools. In the experiment replicas of these were used to cut a block weighing about 2.5 tonnes (the average block size used for the Great Pyramid). It took four workers 4 days (with each working 6 hours a day) to excavate it. The initially slow progress sped up six times when the stone was wetted with water. Based on the data, Burgos extrapolates that about 3,500 quarry-men could have produced the 250 blocks/day needed to complete the Great Pyramid in 27 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>A construction management study conducted in 1999, in association with <a href="/wiki/Mark_Lehner" title="Mark Lehner">Mark Lehner</a> and other Egyptologists, had estimated that the total project required an average workforce of about 13,200 people and a peak workforce of roughly 40,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-civilengineer2_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-civilengineer2-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surveys_and_design">Surveys and design</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Comparison_of_pyramids_SMIL.svg"><img alt="Outlines of various pyramids overlaid on top of on another to show relative height" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Comparison_of_pyramids.svg/langku-280px-Comparison_of_pyramids.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Comparison_of_pyramids.svg/langku-420px-Comparison_of_pyramids.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Comparison_of_pyramids.svg/langku-560px-Comparison_of_pyramids.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparison_of_pyramids.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of approximate profiles of the Great Pyramid of Giza with some notable pyramidal or near-pyramidal buildings. Dotted lines indicate original heights, where data is available. In <a class="external text" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Comparison_of_pyramids_SMIL.svg">its SVG file</a>, hover over a pyramid to highlight and click for its article.</div></div></div>
<p>The first precise measurements of the pyramid were made by Egyptologist <a href="/wiki/Flinders_Petrie" title="Flinders Petrie">Flinders Petrie</a> in 1880–1882, published as <i>The Pyramids and Temples of Gizeh</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883-90">[89]</a></sup> Many of the casing-stones and inner chamber blocks of the Great Pyramid fit together with high precision, with joints, on average, only 0.5 millimetres (0.020 in) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> On the contrary, core blocks were only roughly shaped, with rubble inserted between larger gaps. Mortar was used to bind the outer layers together and fill gaps and joints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fabric_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fabric-6">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The block height and weight tends to get progressively smaller towards the top. Petrie measured the lowest layer to be 148 centimetres (4.86 ft) high, whereas the layers towards the summit barely exceed 50 centimetres (1.6 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The accuracy of the pyramid's perimeter is such that the four sides of the base have an average error of only 58 millimetres (2.3 inches) in length<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[b]</a></sup> and the finished base was squared to a mean corner error of only 12 <a href="/wiki/Minute_and_second_of_arc" title="Minute and second of arc">seconds of arc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie188338_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie188338-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The completed design dimensions are measured to have originally been 280 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_cubit" title="Royal cubit">royal cubits</a> (146.7 m; 481.4 ft) high by 440 cubits (230.6 m; 756.4 ft) long at each of the four sides of its base. Ancient Egyptians used <a href="/wiki/Seked" title="Seked">seked</a> – how much run for one cubit of rise – to describe slopes. For the Great Pyramid a seked of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac" role="math">5<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="tion"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></span> palms was chosen, a ratio of 14 up to 11 in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997218_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997218-95">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some Egyptologists suggest this slope was chosen because the ratio of perimeter to height (1760/280 cubits) equals 2<a href="/wiki/Pi" title="Pi">π</a> to an accuracy of better than 0.05 percent (corresponding to the well-known approximation of π as 22/7). Verner wrote, "We can conclude that although the ancient Egyptians could not precisely define the value of π, in practice they used it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVerner200370_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVerner200370-96">[94]</a></sup> Petrie concluded: "but these relations of areas and of circular ratio are so systematic that we should grant that they were in the builder's design".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie194030_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie194030-97">[95]</a></sup> Others have argued that the ancient Egyptians had no concept of pi and would not have thought to encode it in their monuments and that the observed pyramid slope may be based on the <a href="/wiki/Seked" title="Seked">seked</a> choice alone.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTERossi2007[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_August_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(August_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_98-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTERossi2007[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_August_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(August_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-98'>[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alignment_to_the_cardinal_directions">Alignment to the cardinal directions</span></h4>
<p>The sides of the Great Pyramid's base are closely aligned to the four geographic (not magnetic) cardinal directions, deviating on average <a href="/wiki/Minute_and_second_of_arc" title="Minute and second of arc">3 minutes and 38 seconds of arc</a>, or about a tenth of a <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degree</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Several methods have been proposed for how the ancient Egyptians achieved this level of accuracy:
</p>
<ul><li>The Solar Gnomon Method: The shadow of a vertical rod is tracked throughout a day. The shadow line is intersected by a circle drawn around the base of the rod. Connecting the intersecting points produces an east–west line. An experiment using this method resulted in lines being, on average, 2 minutes, 9 seconds off due east–west. Employing a pinhole produced much more accurate results (19 arc seconds off), whereas using an angled block as a shadow definer was less accurate (3′ 47″ off).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Pole Star Method: The polar star is tracked using a movable sight and fixed plumb line. Halfway between the maximum eastern and western elongations is true north. <a href="/wiki/Thuban" title="Thuban">Thuban</a>, the polar star during the Old Kingdom, was about two degrees removed from the celestial pole at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li>The Simultaneous Transit Method: The stars <a href="/wiki/Mizar" title="Mizar">Mizar</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kochab" title="Kochab">Kochab</a> appear on a vertical line on the horizon, close to true north around 2500 BC. They slowly and simultaneously shift east over time, which is used to explain the relative misalignment of the pyramids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Construction_theories">Construction theories</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramid_construction_techniques" title="Egyptian pyramid construction techniques">Egyptian pyramid construction techniques</a></div>
<p>Many alternative, often contradictory, theories have been proposed regarding the pyramid's construction techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> One mystery of the pyramid's construction is its planning. <a href="/wiki/John_Romer_(Egyptologist)" title="John Romer (Egyptologist)">John Romer</a> suggests that they used the same method that had been used for earlier and later constructions, laying out parts of the plan on the ground at a 1-to-1 scale. He writes that "such a working diagram would also serve to generate the architecture of the pyramid with precision unmatched by any other means".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomer2007327,_329–337_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomer2007327,_329–337-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The basalt blocks of the pyramid temple show "clear evidence" of having been cut with some kind of saw with an estimated cutting blade of 15 feet (4.6 m) in length. Romer suggests that this "super saw" may have had copper teeth and weighed up to 140 kilograms (310 lb). He theorizes that such a saw could have been attached to a wooden <a href="/wiki/Trestle_support" title="Trestle support">trestle support</a> and possibly used in conjunction with vegetable oil, cutting sand, <a href="/wiki/Emery_(rock)" title="Emery (rock)">emery</a> or pounded quartz to cut the blocks, which would have required the labour of at least a dozen men to operate it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERomer2007164,_165_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERomer2007164,_165-106">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exterior">Exterior</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casing">Casing</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="667" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg/220px-Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg/330px-Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg/440px-Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheops_pyramid_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remaining casing stones on the north side of the Great Pyramid</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1988" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg/220px-KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg/330px-KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg/440px-KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KhufuPyramidCasingStone-BritishMuseum-August19-08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Casing stone in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup></div></div></div><p>At completion, the Great Pyramid was cased entirely in white limestone. Precisely worked blocks were placed in horizontal layers and carefully fitted together with mortar, their outward faces cut at a slope and smoothed to a high degree. Together they created four uniform surfaces, angled at 51°50'40" (a <a href="/wiki/Seked" title="Seked">Seked</a> of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac" role="math">5<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="tion"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Palm_(unit)#Ancient_Egypt" title="Palm (unit)">palms</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> Unfinished casing blocks of the pyramids of <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Menkaure" title="Pyramid of Menkaure">Menkaure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_G1-c" title="Pyramid G1-c">Henutsen</a> at Giza suggest that the front faces were smoothed only after the stones were laid, with chiseled seams marking correct positioning and where the superfluous rock would have to be trimmed off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997212–213_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997212–213-110">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The height of the horizontal layers is not uniform but varies considerably. The highest of the 203 remaining courses are towards the bottom, the first layer being the tallest at 1.49 metres (4.9 ft). Towards the top, layers tend to be only slightly over 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_cubit" title="Royal cubit">royal cubit</a> (0.5 m; 1.7 ft) in height. An irregular pattern is noticeable when looking at the sizes in sequence, where layer height declines steadily only to rise sharply again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883vii_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie1883vii-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>So-called "backing stones" supported the casing which were (unlike core blocks) precisely dressed as well and bound to the casing with mortar. Nowadays, these stones give the structure its visible appearance, following the dismantling of the pyramid in the middle ages. In 1303 AD, a <a href="/wiki/1303_Crete_earthquake" title="1303 Crete earthquake">massive earthquake</a> had loosened many of the outer casing stones,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which were said to have been carted away by <a href="/wiki/Bahri_dynasty" title="Bahri dynasty">Bahri Sultan</a> An-Nasir Nasir-ad-Din al-Hasan in 1356 for use in nearby <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie188338_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie188338-94">[92]</a></sup> Many more casing stones were removed from the site by Muhammad Ali Pasha in the early 19th century to build the upper portion of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alabaster_Mosque" title="Alabaster Mosque">Alabaster Mosque</a> in Cairo, not far from Giza.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Later explorers reported massive piles of rubble at the base of the pyramids left over from the continuing collapse of the casing stones, which were subsequently cleared away during continuing excavations of the site. Today a few of the casing stones from the lowest course can be seen <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_situ_conservation_(archaeology)" title="In situ conservation (archaeology)">in situ</a></i> on each side, with the best preserved on the north below the entrances, excavated by Vyse in 1837.
</p><p>The mortar was chemically analyzed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> and contains organic inclusions (mostly charcoal), samples of which were radiocarbon dated to 2871–2604 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> It has been theorized that the mortar enabled the masons to set the stones exactly by providing a level bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkeEngelbach199178–79_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkeEngelbach199178–79-116">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStocks2003182–183_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStocks2003182–183-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been suggested that some or all of the casing stones were cast in place, rather than quarried and moved, yet archaeological evidence and petrographic analysis indicate this was not the case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Petrie noted in 1880 that the sides of the pyramid, as we see them today, are "very distinctly hollowed" and that "each side has a sort of groove specially down the middle of the face", which he reasoned was a result of increased casing thickness in these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie188343–44_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie188343–44-119">[117]</a></sup> A laser scanning survey in 2005 confirmed the existence of the anomalies, which can be, to some degree, attributed to damaged and removed stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> Under certain lighting conditions and with image enhancement the faces can appear to be split, leading to speculation that the pyramid had been intentionally constructed eight-sided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pyramidion_and_missing_tip">Pyramidion and missing tip</span></h3>
<p>The pyramid was once topped by a capstone known as a <a href="/wiki/Pyramidion" title="Pyramidion">pyramidion</a>. The material it was made from is subject to much speculation; limestone, granite or basalt are commonly proposed, while in popular culture it is often solid gold or gilded. All known 4th dynasty <i>pyramidia</i> (of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Pyramid" title="Red Pyramid">Red Pyramid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_G1-d" title="Pyramid G1-d">Satellite Pyramid of Khufu</a> (G1-d) and Queen's Pyramid of Menkaure (G3-a)) are of white limestone and were not gilded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> Only from the 5th dynasty onward is there evidence of gilded capstones; for instance, a scene on the causeway of the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Sahure" title="Pyramid of Sahure">Sahure</a> speaks of the "white gold pyramidion of the pyramid Sahure’s Soul Shines".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Great Pyramid's pyramidion was already lost in antiquity, as Pliny the Elder and later authors report of a platform on its summit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pliny_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pliny-60">[59]</a></sup> Nowadays the pyramid is about 8 metres (26 ft) shorter than it was when intact, with about 1,000 tonnes of material missing from the top. 
</p><p>In 1874 a mast was installed on the top by the Scottish astronomer <a href="/wiki/David_Gill_(astronomer)" title="David Gill (astronomer)">Sir David Gill</a> who, whilst returning from work involving observing a rare <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">Venus transit</a>, was invited to survey Egypt and began by surveying the Great Pyramid. His results measuring the pyramid were accurate to within 1 mm and the survey mast is still in place to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">Interior</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1950" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg/300px-Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg/450px-Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg/600px-Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Pyramid_S-N_Diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elevation diagram of the interior structures of the Great Pyramid. The inner and outer lines indicate the pyramid's present and original profiles. <table> <tbody><tr> <td><b>1.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Original_entrance" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Original entrance</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>2.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Robbers'_Tunnel" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Robbers' Tunnel</a> (tourist entrance) </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>3, 4.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Descending_Passage" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Descending Passage</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>5.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Subterranean_Chamber" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Subterranean Chamber</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>6.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Ascending_Passage" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Ascending Passage</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>7.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Queen's_Chamber" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Queen's Chamber</a> &amp; its "air-shafts" </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>8.</b> Horizontal Passage </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>9.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Grand_Gallery" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Grand Gallery</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>10.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#King's_Chamber" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">King's Chamber</a> &amp; its <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Air_shafts" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">"air-shafts"</a> </td></tr> <tr> <td><b>11.</b> <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza#Well_Shaft_and_Grotto" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Grotto &amp; Well Shaft</a> </td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><p>The internal structure consists of three main chambers (the King's-, Queen's- and Subterranean Chamber), the Grand Gallery and various corridors and shafts.
</p><p>There are two entrances into the pyramid: the original and a forced passage, which meet at a junction. From there, one passage descends into the Subterranean Chamber, while the other ascends to the Grand Gallery. From the beginning of the gallery three paths can be taken:
</p>
<ul><li>a vertical shaft that leads down, past a grotto, to meet the descending passage,</li>
<li>a horizontal corridor leading to the Queen's Chamber,</li>
<li>and the path up the gallery itself to the King's Chamber that contains the sarcophagus.</li></ul>
<p>Both the King's and Queen's Chamber have a pair of small "air-shafts". Above the King's Chamber are a series of five Relieving Chambers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entrances">Entrances</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheops-Pyramid@Eingaenge.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2657" data-file-width="1771" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Cheops-Pyramid%40Eingaenge.JPG/220px-Cheops-Pyramid%40Eingaenge.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Cheops-Pyramid%40Eingaenge.JPG/330px-Cheops-Pyramid%40Eingaenge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Cheops-Pyramid%40Eingaenge.JPG/440px-Cheops-Pyramid%40Eingaenge.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheops-Pyramid@Eingaenge.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Original Entrance (Top-Left), Robbers' Tunnel (Middle-Right)</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_entrance">Original entrance</span></h4>
<p>The original entrance is located on the north side, 15 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_cubit" title="Royal cubit">royal cubits</a> (7.9 m; 25.8 ft) east of the centerline of the pyramid. Before the removal of the casing in the middle ages, the pyramid was entered through a hole in the 19th layer of masonry, approximately 17 metres (56 ft) above the pyramid's base level. The height of that layer – 96 centimetres (3.15 ft) – corresponds to the size of the entrance tunnel which is commonly called the Descending Passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase2004b15_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase2004b15-126">[124]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> (64–24 BC) a movable stone could be raised to enter this sloping corridor, however it is not known if it was a later addition or original.
</p><p>A row of double chevrons diverts weight away from the entrance. Several of these chevron blocks are now missing, as the slanted faces they used to rest on indicate.
</p><p>Numerous, mostly modern, graffiti is cut into the stones around the entrance. Most notable is a large, square text of hieroglyphs carved in honor of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_IV_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William IV of Prussia">Frederick William IV</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Richard_Lepsius" title="Karl Richard Lepsius">Karl Richard Lepsius</a>'s Prussian expedition to Egypt in 1842.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Face_Corridor">North Face Corridor</span></h5>
<p>In 2016 the <a href="/wiki/Scanpyramids" title="Scanpyramids">ScanPyramids</a> team detected a cavity behind the entrance chevrons using <a href="/wiki/Muography" title="Muography">muography</a>, which was confirmed in 2019 to be a corridor at least 5 metres (16 ft) long, running horizontal or sloping upwards (thus not parallel to the Descending Passage).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup> Whether or not it connects to the Big Void above the Grand Gallery remains to be seen.
</p>
<h4><span id="Robbers.27_Tunnel"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Robbers'_Tunnel">Robbers' Tunnel</span></h4>
<p>Today tourists enter the Great Pyramid via the Robbers' Tunnel, which was long ago cut straight through the masonry of the pyramid. The entrance was forced into the 6th and 7th layer of the casing, about 7 metres (23 ft) above the base. After running more-or-less straight and horizontal for 27 metres (89 ft) it turns sharply left to encounter the blocking stones in the Ascending Passage. It is possible to enter the Descending Passage from this point but access is usually forbidden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley200738–40_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley200738–40-130">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>The origin of this Robbers' Tunnel is the subject of much scholarly discussion. According to tradition the chasm was made around 820 AD by Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">al-Ma'mun</a>'s workmen with a battering ram. The digging dislodged the stone in the ceiling of the Descending Passage which hid the entrance to the Ascending Passage, and the noise of that stone falling then sliding down the Descending Passage alerted them to the need to turn left. Unable to remove these stones, however, the workmen tunneled up beside them through the softer limestone of the Pyramid until they reached the Ascending Passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley200738_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley200738-131">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBattutah200218_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBattutah200218-132">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to a number of historical and archaeological discrepancies, many scholars (with <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Isaac_Silvestre_de_Sacy" title="Antoine Isaac Silvestre de Sacy">Antoine de Sacy</a> perhaps being the first) contend that this story is apocryphal. They argue that it is much more likely that the tunnel had been carved shortly after the pyramid was initially sealed. This tunnel, the scholars continue, was then resealed (likely during the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">Ramesside Restoration</a>), and it was this plug that al-Ma'mun's ninth-century expedition cleared away.
