This theory is furthered by the report of patriarch <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_I_Telmaharoyo" title="Dionysius I Telmaharoyo">Dionysius I Telmaharoyo</a>, who claimed that before al-Ma'mun's expedition, there already existed a breach in the pyramid's north face that extended into the structure 33 metres (108 ft) before hitting a dead end. This suggests that some sort of robber's tunnel predated al-Ma'mun, and that the caliph simply enlarged it and cleared it of debris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooperson2010170–175_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooperson2010170–175-133">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Descending_Passage">Descending Passage</span></h3>
<p>From the original entrance, a passage descends through the masonry of the pyramid and then into the bedrock beneath it, ultimately leading to the Subterranean Chamber.
</p><p>It has a slanted height of 4 <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_units_of_measurement" title="Ancient Egyptian units of measurement">Egyptian feet</a> (1.20 m; 3.9 ft) and a width of 2 cubits (1.0 m; 3.4 ft). Its angle of 26°26'46" corresponds to a ratio of 1 to 2 (rise over run).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004284_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004284-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>After 28 metres (92 ft), the lower end of the Ascending Passage is reached; a <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:46_edgar.jpg" title="c:File:46 edgar.jpg">square hole</a> in the ceiling, which is blocked by granite stones and might have originally been concealed. To circumvent these hard stones, a short tunnel was excavated that meets the end of the Robbers' Tunnel. This was expanded over time and fitted with stairs.
</p><p>The passage continues to descend for another 72 metres (236 ft), now through bedrock instead of the pyramid superstructure. Lazy guides used to block off this part with rubble to avoid having to lead people down and back up the long shaft, until around 1902 when <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lorenzo_Dow_Covington&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lorenzo Dow Covington (page does not exist)">Covington</a> installed a padlocked iron grill-door to stop this practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdgarEdgar1910141_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdgarEdgar1910141-135">[133]</a></sup> Near the end of this section, on the west wall, is the connection to the vertical shaft that leads up to the Grand Gallery.
</p><p>A horizontal shaft connects the end of the Descending Passage to the Subterranean Chamber, It has a length of 8.84 m (29.0 ft), width of 85 cm (2.79 ft) and height of 91–95 cm (2.99–3.12 ft). A <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:32_edgar.jpg" title="c:File:32 edgar.jpg">recess</a> is located towards the end of the western wall, slightly larger than the tunnel, the ceiling of which is irregular and undressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a30_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a30-136">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subterranean_Chamber">Subterranean Chamber</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:27_edgar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="659" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/27_edgar.jpg/200px-27_edgar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/27_edgar.jpg/300px-27_edgar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/27_edgar.jpg/400px-27_edgar.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:27_edgar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Subterranean Chamber (looking west) in 1909 with rubble from the Pit Shaft excavation still filling the chamber.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:30_edgar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/30_edgar.jpg/200px-30_edgar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/30_edgar.jpg/300px-30_edgar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/30_edgar.jpg/400px-30_edgar.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:30_edgar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Subterranean Chamber (looking south) with Pit Shaft in the floor and blind corridor entrance.</div></div></div>
<p>The Subterranean Chamber, or "Pit", is the lowest of the three main chambers and the only one dug into the bedrock beneath the pyramid.
</p><p>Located about 27 m (89 ft) below base level,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup> it measures roughly 16 cubits (8.4 m; 27.5 ft) north-south by 27 cubits (14.1 m; 46.4 ft) east-west, with an approximate height of 4 m (13 ft).
</p><p>The western half of the room, apart from the ceiling, is unfinished, with trenches left behind by the quarry-men running east to west. A <a class="external text" href="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/28_edgar.jpg">niche</a> was cut into the northern half of the west wall. The only <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:31_edgar.jpg" title="c:File:31 edgar.jpg">access</a>, through the Descending Passage, lies on the eastern end of the north wall.
</p><p>Although seemingly known in antiquity, according to Herodotus and later authors, its existence had been forgotten in the middle ages until rediscovery in 1817, when <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Caviglia" title="Giovanni Battista Caviglia">Giovanni Caviglia</a> cleared the rubble blocking the Descending Passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerring18393,_Plate_IX_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerring18393,_Plate_IX-137">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Opposing the entrance, a blind corridor runs straight south for 11 m (36 ft) and continues slight bent another 5.4 m (18 ft), measuring about 0.75 m (2.5 ft) squared. A Greek or Roman character was found on its ceiling with the light of a candle, suggesting that the chamber had indeed been accessible during <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b290_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b290-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the middle of the eastern half, a large hole is opened up, called Pit Shaft or <a href="/wiki/John_Shae_Perring" title="John Shae Perring">Perring</a>'s Shaft. The upmost part may have ancient origins, about 2 m (6.6 ft) squared in width and 1.5 m (4.9 ft) in depth, diagonally aligned with the chamber. Caviglia and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Salt_(Egyptologist)" title="Henry Salt (Egyptologist)">Salt</a> enlarged it to the depth of about 3 m (9.8 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerring1839_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerring1839-139">[137]</a></sup> In 1837 <a href="/wiki/Howard_Vyse" title="Howard Vyse">Vyse</a> directed the shaft to be sunk to a depth of 50 ft (15 m), in hopes of discovering the chamber encompassed by water that Herodotus alludes to. It is slightly narrower in width at about 1.5 m (4.9 ft). No chamber was discovered after Perring and his workers had spent one and a half years penetrating the bedrock to the then water level of the Nile, some 12 m (39 ft) further down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyse1840a223–224_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyse1840a223–224-140">[138]</a></sup> The rubble produced during this operation was deposited throughout the chamber. Petrie, visiting in 1880, found the shaft to be partially filled with rainwater that had rushed down the Descending Passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie188360_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie188360-141">[139]</a></sup> In 1909, when the Edgar brothers' surveying activities were encumbered by the material, they moved the sand and smaller stones back into the shaft, leaving the upper part clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdgarEdgar1910147_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdgarEdgar1910147-142">[140]</a></sup> The deep, modern shaft is sometimes mistaken to be part of the original design.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Borchardt" title="Ludwig Borchardt">Ludwig Borchardt</a> suggested that the Subterranean Chamber was originally planned to be the burial place for pharaoh Khufu, but that it was abandoned during construction in favour of a chamber higher up in the pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965148_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965148-143">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ascending_Passage">Ascending Passage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:49_edgar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="667" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/49_edgar.jpg/220px-49_edgar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/49_edgar.jpg/330px-49_edgar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/49_edgar.jpg/440px-49_edgar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:49_edgar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The upper two granite plugs in the Ascending Passage, seen from the end of the Robbers' Tunnel</div></div></div>
<p>The Ascending Passage connects the Descending Passage to the Grand Gallery. It is 75 cubits (39.3 m; 128.9 ft) long and of the same width and height as the shaft it originates from, although its angle is slightly lower at 26°6'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004286_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004286-144">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>The lower end of the shaft is plugged by three granite stones, which were slid down from the Grand Gallery to seal the tunnel. They are 1.57 m (5.2 ft), 1.67 m (5.5 ft) and 1 m (3.3 ft) long respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004286_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004286-144">[142]</a></sup> The uppermost is heavily damaged, hence it is shorter. The end of the Robbers' Tunnel concludes slightly below the stones, so a short tunnel was dug around them to gain access to the Descending Passage, since the surrounding limestone is considerably softer and easier to work.
</p><p>Most of the joints between the blocks of the walls run perpendicular to the floor, with two exceptions. Firstly, those in the lower third of the corridor are vertical. Secondly, the three girdle stones that are inserted near the middle (about 10 cubits apart), presumably to stabilize the tunnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a114-115_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a114-115-145">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Well_Shaft_and_Grotto">Well Shaft and Grotto</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:39_edgar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="659" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/39_edgar.jpg/220px-39_edgar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/39_edgar.jpg/330px-39_edgar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/39_edgar.jpg/440px-39_edgar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:39_edgar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grotto (left) accessed through the broken wall of the Well Shaft (right)</div></div></div>
<p>The Well Shaft (also known as the Service Shaft or Vertical Shaft) links the lower end of the Grand Gallery to the bottom of Descending Passage, about 50 metres (160 ft) further down.
</p><p>It takes a winding and indirect course. The upper half goes through the nucleus masonry of the pyramid. It runs vertical at first for 8 metres (26 ft), then slightly angled southwards for about the same distance, until it hits bedrock approximately 5.7 metres (19 ft) above the pyramid's base level. Another vertical section descends further, which is partially lined with masonry that has been broken through to a cavity known as the Grotto. The lower half of the Well Shaft goes through the bedrock at an angle of about 45° for 26.5 metres (87 ft) before a steeper section, 9.5 metres (31 ft) long, leads to its lowest point. The final section of 2.6 metres (8.5 ft) connects it to the Descending Passage, running almost horizontal. The builders evidently had trouble aligning the lower exit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase2004b_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase2004b-146">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The purpose of the shaft is commonly explained as a ventilation shaft for the Subterranean Chamber and as an escape shaft for the workers who slid the blocking stones of the Ascending Passage into place.
</p><p>The Grotto is a natural limestone cave that was likely filled with sand and gravel before construction, before being hollowed out by looters. A granite block rests in it that likely originated from the portcullis that once sealed the King's Chamber.
</p>
<h3><span id="Queen.27s_Chamber"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Queen's_Chamber">Queen's Chamber</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2300" data-file-width="1668" decoding="async" height="303" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg/220px-Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg/330px-Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg/440px-Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chambre-reine-kheops.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Axonometric view of the Queen's Chamber</div></div></div>
<p>The Horizontal Passage links the Grand Gallery to the Queen's Chamber. Five pairs of holes at the start suggest the tunnel was once concealed with slabs that laid flush with the gallery floor. The passage is 2 cubits (1.0 m; 3.4 ft) wide and 1.17 m (3.8 ft) high for most of its length, but near the chamber there is a step in the floor, after which the passage increases to 1.68 m (5.5 ft) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup> Half of the west-wall consists of two layers that have atypically continuous vertical joints. Dormion suggests the entrances to magazines laid here and have been filled in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004119–124_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004119–124-147">[145]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Queen's Chamber is exactly halfway between the north and south faces of the pyramid. It measures 10 cubits (5.2 m; 17.2 ft) north-south, 11 cubits (5.8 m; 18.9 ft) east-west,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004259_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004259-148">[146]</a></sup> and has a pointed roof that apexes at 12 cubits (6.3 m; 20.6 ft) tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004154_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004154-149">[147]</a></sup> At the eastern end of the chamber there is a <a href="/wiki/Niche_(architecture)" title="Niche (architecture)">niche</a> 9 cubits (4.7 m; 15.5 ft) high. The original depth of the niche was 2 cubits (1.0 m; 3.4 ft), but it has since been deepened by treasure hunters.
</p><p>Shafts were discovered in the north and south walls of the Queen's Chamber in 1872 by British engineer <a href="/wiki/Waynman_Dixon" title="Waynman Dixon">Waynman Dixon</a>, who believed shafts similar to those in the King's Chamber must also exist. The shafts were not connected to the outer faces of the pyramid or the Queen's Chamber; their purpose is unknown. In one shaft Dixon discovered a ball of <a href="/wiki/Diorite" title="Diorite">diorite</a>, a bronze hook of unknown purpose and a piece of cedar wood. The first two objects are currently in the British Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheops_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-cheops-150">[148]</a></sup> The latter was lost until recently when it was found at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a>. It has since been radiocarbon dated to 3341–3094 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> The northern shaft's angle of ascent fluctuates and at one point turns 45 degrees to avoid the Great Gallery. The southern shaft is perpendicular to the pyramid's slope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cheops_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-cheops-150">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The shafts in the Queen's Chamber were explored in 1993 by the German engineer Rudolf Gantenbrink using a crawler robot he designed, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upuaut_2" title="Upuaut 2">Upuaut 2</a></i>. After a climb of 65 m (213 ft),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup> he discovered that one of the shafts was blocked by a limestone "door" with two eroded copper "handles". The <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a> created a similar robot which, in September 2002, drilled a small hole in the southern door only to find another stone slab behind it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> The northern passage, which was difficult to navigate because of its twists and turns, was also found to be blocked by a slab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research continued in 2011 with the <a href="/wiki/Djedi_Project" title="Djedi Project">Djedi Project</a> which used a fibre-optic "<a href="/wiki/Fiberscope" title="Fiberscope">micro snake camera</a>" that could see around corners. With this, they were able to penetrate the first door of the southern shaft through the hole drilled in 2002, and view all the sides of the small chamber behind it. They discovered hieroglyphics written in red paint. Egyptian mathematics researcher Luca Miatello stated that the markings read "121" – the length of the shaft in cubits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[153]</a></sup> The Djedi team were also able to scrutinize the inside of the two copper "handles" embedded in the door, which they now believe to be for decorative purposes. They additionally found the reverse side of the "door" to be finished and polished, which suggests that it was not put there just to block the shaft from debris, but rather for a more specific reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grand_Gallery">Grand Gallery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grande-galerie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Grande-galerie.jpg/220px-Grande-galerie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Grande-galerie.jpg/330px-Grande-galerie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Grande-galerie.jpg/440px-Grande-galerie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grande-galerie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Grand Gallery (with modern walkway up the middle)</div></div></div>
<p>The Grand Gallery continues the slope of the Ascending Passage towards the King's Chamber, extending from the 23rd to the 48th <a href="/wiki/Course_(architecture)" title="Course (architecture)">course</a>, a rise of 21 metres (69 ft). It has been praised as a "truly spectacular example of stonemasonry".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_2018_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2018-157">[155]</a></sup> It is 8.6 metres (28 ft) high and 46.68 metres (153.1 ft) long. The base is 4 cubits (2.1 m; 6.9 ft) wide, but after two courses – at a height of 2.29 metres (7.5 ft) – the blocks of stone in the walls are <a href="/wiki/Corbel" title="Corbel">corbelled</a> inwards by 6–10 centimetres (2.4–3.9 in) on each side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup> There are seven of these steps, so, at the top, the Grand Gallery is only 2 cubits (1.0 m; 3.4 ft) wide. It is roofed by slabs of stone laid at a slightly steeper angle than the floor so that each stone fits into a slot cut into the top of the gallery, like the teeth of a <a href="/wiki/Ratchet_(device)" title="Ratchet (device)">ratchet</a>. The purpose was to have each block supported by the wall of the Gallery, rather than resting on the block beneath it, in order to prevent cumulative pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKingsland1932[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOieJ8015ODECpgPA71_71]_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKingsland1932[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOieJ8015ODECpgPA71_71]-158">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the upper end of the Gallery, on the eastern wall, there is a hole near the roof that opens into a short tunnel by which access can be gained to the lowest of the Relieving Chambers.
</p><p>The floor of the Grand Gallery has a shelf or step on either side, 1 cubit (52.4 cm; 20.6 in) wide, leaving a lower ramp 2 cubits (1.0 m; 3.4 ft) wide between them. There are 56 slots on the shelves, with 28 on each side. On each wall, 25 niches have been cut above the slots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[157]</a></sup> The purpose of these slots is not known, but the central gutter in the floor of the Gallery, which is the same width as the Ascending Passage, has led to speculation that the blocking stones were stored in the Grand Gallery and the slots held wooden beams to restrain them from sliding down the passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELehner1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailscompletepyramids00lehnpage113_113]_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELehner1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailscompletepyramids00lehnpage113_113]-160">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Houdin" title="Jean-Pierre Houdin">Jean-Pierre Houdin</a> theorized that they held a timber frame that was used in combination with a trolley to pull the heavy granite blocks up the pyramid.
</p><p>At the top of the gallery, there is a step onto a small horizontal platform where a tunnel leads through the Antechamber, once blocked by portcullis stones, into the King's Chamber.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Big_Void">The Big Void</span></h3>
<p>In 2017, scientists from the <a href="/wiki/Scanpyramids" title="Scanpyramids">ScanPyramids</a> project discovered a large cavity above the Grand Gallery using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muon_radiography" title="Muon radiography">muon radiography</a>, which they called the "ScanPyramids Big Void". Key was a research team under Morishima Kunihiro, a professor at <a href="/wiki/Nagoya_University" title="Nagoya University">Nagoya University</a>, that used special nuclear emulsion detectors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[160]</a></sup> Its length is at least 30 metres (98 ft) and its cross-section is similar to that of the Grand Gallery. Its existence was confirmed by independent detection with three different technologies: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_emulsion" title="Nuclear emulsion">nuclear emulsion</a> films, <a href="/wiki/Scintillator" title="Scintillator">scintillator</a> <a href="/wiki/Hodoscope" title="Hodoscope">hodoscopes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas_detector" title="Gas detector">gas detectors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[162]</a></sup> The purpose of the cavity is unknown and it is not accessible. <a href="/wiki/Zahi_Hawass" title="Zahi Hawass">Zahi Hawass</a> speculates it may have been a gap used in the construction of the Grand Gallery,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[163]</a></sup> but the Japanese research team state that the void is completely different from previously identified construction spaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>To verify and pinpoint the void, a team from Kyushu University, Tohoku University, the University of Tokyo and the Chiba Institute of Technology planned to rescan the structure with a newly developed muon detector in 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[165]</a></sup> Their work was delayed by the <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic" title="COVID-19 pandemic">COVID-19 pandemic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antechamber">Antechamber</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piazzi-plate_12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="744" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Piazzi-plate_12.jpg/220px-Piazzi-plate_12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Piazzi-plate_12.jpg/330px-Piazzi-plate_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Piazzi-plate_12.jpg/440px-Piazzi-plate_12.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piazzi-plate_12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the Antechamber</div></div></div>
<p>The last line of defense against intrusion was a small chamber specially designed to house portcullis blocking stones, called the Antechamber. It is cased almost entirely in granite and is situated between the upper end of the Grand Gallery and the King's Chamber. Three slots for portcullis stones line the east and west wall of the chamber. Each of them is topped with a semi-circular groove for a log, around which ropes could be spanned.
</p><p>The granite portcullis stones were approximately 1 cubit (52.4 cm; 20.6 in) thick and were lowered into position by the aforementioned ropes which were tied through a series of four holes at the top of the blocks. A corresponding set of four vertical grooves are on the south wall of the chamber, recesses that make space for the ropes.
</p><p>The Antechamber has a design flaw: the space above them can be accessed, thus all but the last block can be circumvented. This was exploited by looters who punched a hole through the ceiling of the tunnel behind, gaining access to the King's Chamber. Later on, all three portcullis stones were broken and removed. Fragments of these blocks can be found in various locations in the pyramid (the Pit Shaft, the Original Entrance, the Grotto and the recess before the Subterranean Chamber).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaase2004b_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaase2004b-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="King.27s_Chamber"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="King's_Chamber">King's Chamber</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2347" data-file-width="1543" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg/220px-Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg/330px-Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg/440px-Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kheops-chambre-roi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Axonometric view of the King's Chamber</div></div></div>
<p>The King's Chamber is the upmost of the three main chambers of the pyramid. It is faced entirely with <a href="/wiki/Granite" title="Granite">granite</a> and measures 20 cubits (10.5 m; 34.4 ft) east-west by 10 cubits (5.2 m; 17.2 ft) north-south. Its flat ceiling is about 11 cubits and 5 digits (5.8 m;19.0 ft) above the floor, formed by nine slabs of stone weighing in total about 400 tons. All the roof beams show cracks due to the chamber having settled 2.5–5 cm (0.98–1.97 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a48_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a48-169">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>The walls consist of five courses of blocks that are uninscribed, as was the norm for burial chambers of the 4th dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[168]</a></sup> The stones are precisely fitted together. The facing surfaces are dressed to varying degrees, with some displaying remains of <a href="/wiki/Boss_(architecture)" title="Boss (architecture)">bosses</a> not entirely cut away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a48_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a48-169">[167]</a></sup> The back sides of the blocks were only roughly hewn to shape, as was usual with Egyptian hard-stone facade blocks, presumably to save work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b_80-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965b-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sarcophagus">Sarcophagus</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg/220px-Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg/330px-Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg/440px-Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chambre-roi-grande-pyramide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sarcophagus in the King's Chamber</div></div></div>
<p>The only object in the King's Chamber is a <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> made out of a single, hollowed-out granite block. When it was rediscovered in the early middle ages, it was found broken open and any contents had already been removed. It is of the form common for early Egyptian sarcophagi, rectangular in shape with grooves to slide the now missing lid into place with three small holes for pegs to fixate it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie188384_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie188384-172">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a50_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a50-173">[171]</a></sup> The coffer was not perfectly smoothed, displaying various tool marks matching those of copper saws and tubular hand-drills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStocks2003_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStocks2003-174">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>The internal dimensions are roughly 198 cm (6.50 ft) by 68 cm (2.23 feet), the external 228 cm (7.48 ft) by 98 cm (3.22 ft), with a height of 105 cm (3.44 ft). The walls have a thickness of about 15 cm (0.49 ft). The sarcophagus is too large to fit around the corner between the Ascending and Descending Passages, which indicates that it must have been placed in the chamber before the roof was put in place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a52_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a52-175">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_shafts">Air shafts</span></h4>
<p>In the north and south walls of the King's Chamber are two narrow shafts, commonly known as "air shafts". They face each other and are located approximately 0.91 m (3.0 ft) above the floor, 2.5 m (8.2 ft) from the eastern wall, with a width of 18 and 21 cm (7.1 and 8.3 in) and a height of 14 cm (5.5 in). Both start out horizontally for the length of the granite blocks they go through before changing to an upwards direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDormion2004296_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDormion2004296-176">[174]</a></sup> The southern shaft ascends at an angle of 45° with a slight curve westwards. One ceiling stone was found to be distinctly unfinished which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Upuaut_Project" title="The Upuaut Project">Gantenbrink</a> called a "Monday morning block". The northern shaft changes angle several times, shifting the path to the west, perhaps to avoid the Big Void. The builders had trouble calculating the right angles, resulting in parts of the shaft being narrower. Nowadays they both commute to the exterior. If they originally penetrated the outer casing is unknown.
</p><p>The purpose of these shafts is not clear: They were long believed by Egyptologists to be shafts for ventilation, but this idea has now been widely abandoned in favour of the shafts serving a ritualistic purpose associated with the ascension of the king's spirit to the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacksonStamp200279,_104_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacksonStamp200279,_104-177">[175]</a></sup> Ironically, both shafts were fitted with ventilators in 1992 to reduce the humidity in the pyramid.
</p><p>The idea that the shafts point towards stars or areas of the northern and southern skies has been largely dismissed as the northern shaft follows a <a href="/wiki/Dog-leg_(stairs)" title="Dog-leg (stairs)">dog-leg course</a> through the masonry and the southern shaft has a bend of approximately 20 centimetres (7.9 in), indicating no intention to have them point to any celestial objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Upuaut_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Upuaut-178">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relieving_chambers">Relieving chambers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piazzi-plate_13.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="724" data-file-width="473" decoding="async" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Piazzi-plate_13.jpg/220px-Piazzi-plate_13.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Piazzi-plate_13.jpg/330px-Piazzi-plate_13.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Piazzi-plate_13.jpg/440px-Piazzi-plate_13.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piazzi-plate_13.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relieving Chambers above the King's Chamber, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Piazzi_Smyth" title="Charles Piazzi Smyth">Smyth</a> 1877</div></div></div>
<p>Above the roof of the King's Chamber are five compartments, named (from lowest upwards) "<a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Davison" title="Nathaniel Davison">Davison</a>'s Chamber", "<a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Wellington</a>'s Chamber", "<a href="/wiki/Horatio_Nelson,_1st_Viscount_Nelson" title="Horatio Nelson, 1st Viscount Nelson">Nelson</a>'s Chamber", "<a href="/wiki/Arbuthnot_baronets" title="Arbuthnot baronets">Lady Arbuthnot</a>'s Chamber", and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patrick_Campbell_(1779%E2%80%931857)" title="Patrick Campbell (1779–1857)">Campbell</a>'s Chamber".
</p><p>They were presumably intended to safeguard the King's Chamber from the possibility of the roof collapsing under the weight of stone above; hence they are referred to as "Relieving Chambers".
</p><p>The granite blocks that divide the chambers have flat bottom sides but roughly shaped top sides, giving all five chambers an irregular floor, but a flat ceiling, with the exception of the uppermost chamber which has a pointed limestone roof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a24_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a24-179">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nathaniel Davison is credited with the discovery of the lowest of these chambers in 1763, although a French merchant named Maynard informed him of its existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b180_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyse1840b180-180">[178]</a></sup> It can be reached through an ancient passage that originates from the top of the south wall of the Grand Gallery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a24_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaragioglioRinaldi1965a24-179">[177]</a></sup> The upper four chambers were discovered in 1837 by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Vyse" title="Howard Vyse">Howard Vyse</a> after discovering a crack in the ceiling of the first chamber. This allowed the insertion of a long reed, which, with the employment of gunpowder and boring rods, forced a tunnel upwards through the masonry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVyse1840a155,_203pp_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVyse1840a155,_203pp-181">[179]</a></sup> As no access shafts existed for the upper four chambers – unlike Davison's Chamber – they were completely inaccessible until this point.
</p><p>Numerous graffiti of red <a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">ochre</a> paint were found to cover the limestone walls of all four newly discovered chambers. Apart from leveling lines and indication marks for masons, multiple hieroglyphic inscriptions spell out the names of work-gangs. Those names, which were also found in other Egyptian pyramids like that of <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Menkaure" title="Pyramid of Menkaure">Menkaure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Sahure" title="Pyramid of Sahure">Sahure</a>, usually included the name of the pharaoh they were working for.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reisner1931_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reisner1931-13">[12]</a></sup> The blocks must have received the inscriptions before the chambers became inaccessible during construction. Their orientation, often side-ways or upside down, and their sometimes being partially covered by blocks, seems to indicate that the stones were inscribed before being laid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerring1839Plates_V–VII_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerring1839Plates_V–VII-183">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>The inscriptions, correctly deciphered only decades after discovery, read as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reisner1931_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reisner1931-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"The gang, The Horus Mededuw-is-the-purifier-of-the-two-lands." Found once in relieving chamber 3. (Mededuw being Khufu's Horus name.)</li>
<li>"The gang, The Horus Mededuw-is-pure" Found seven times in chamber 4.</li>
<li>"The gang, Khufu-excites-love" Found once in chamber 5 (top chamber).</li>
<li>“The gang, The-white-crown-of Khnumkhuwfuw-is-powerful” Found once in chambers 2 and 3, ten times in chamber 4 and twice in chamber 5. (Khnum-Khufu being Khufu's full birth name.)</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pyramid_complex">Pyramid complex</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Giza_pyramid_complex" title="Giza pyramid complex">Giza pyramid complex</a></div>
<p>The Great Pyramid is surrounded by a complex of several buildings, including small pyramids.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temples_and_causeway">Temples and causeway</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="667" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg/220px-Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg/330px-Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg/440px-Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vestiges-temple-kh%C3%A9ops.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Remains of the basalt floor of the temple at the east foot of the pyramid</div></div></div><p>The Pyramid Temple, which stood on the east side of the pyramid and measured 52.2 metres (171 ft) north to south and 40 metres (130 ft) east to west, has almost entirely disappeared. Only some of the black <a href="/wiki/Basalt" title="Basalt">basalt</a> paving has remained. There are only a few remnants of the causeway which linked the pyramid with the valley and the Valley Temple. The Valley Temple is buried beneath the village of Nazlet el-Samman; basalt paving and limestone walls have been found but the site has not been excavated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold200551–52_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold200551–52-184">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldStrudwickStrudwick2002[httpsarchiveorgdetailsencyclopaediaofa00dietpage126_126]_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldStrudwickStrudwick2002[httpsarchiveorgdetailsencyclopaediaofa00dietpage126_126]-185">[183]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_cemetery">East cemetery</span></h3>
<p>The tomb of <a href="/wiki/Hetepheres_I" title="Hetepheres I">Queen Hetepheres I</a>, sister-wife of <a href="/wiki/Sneferu" title="Sneferu">Sneferu</a> and mother of Khufu, is located approximately 110 metres (360 ft) east of the Great Pyramid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[184]</a></sup> Discovered by accident by the Reisner expedition, the burial was intact, although the carefully sealed coffin proved to be empty.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsidiary_pyramids">Subsidiary pyramids</span></h4>
<p>On the southern end of the east side are four subsidiary pyramids The three that remain standing to almost full height are popularly known as the Queens' Pyramids (<a href="/wiki/Pyramid_G1-a" title="Pyramid G1-a">G1-a</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_G1-b" title="Pyramid G1-b">G1-b</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyramid_G1-c" title="Pyramid G1-c">G1-c</a>). The fourth, smaller satellite pyramid (<a href="/wiki/Pyramid_G1-d" title="Pyramid G1-d">G1-d</a>), was so ruined that its existence was not suspected until the first course of stones and, later, the remains of the capstone were discovered during excavations in 1991–93.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boats">Boats</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3048" data-file-width="4064" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg/220px-Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg/330px-Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg/440px-Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giseh_Sonnenbarke_12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Restored Khufu ship displayed at the <a href="/wiki/Giza_Solar_boat_museum" title="Giza Solar boat museum">Giza Solar boat museum</a>, now relocated to the Grand Egyptian Museum</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Khufu_ship" title="Khufu ship">Khufu ship</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solar_barque" title="Solar barque">Solar barque</a></div>
<p>Three boat-shaped pits are located east of the pyramid. They are large enough in size and shape to have held complete boats, though so shallow that any superstructure, if there ever was one, must have been removed or disassembled.
</p><p>Two additional boat pits, long and rectangular in shape, were found south of the pyramid, still covered with slabs of stone weighing up to 15 tons.
</p><p>The first of these was discovered in May 1954 by the Egyptian archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Kamal_el-Mallakh" title="Kamal el-Mallakh">Kamal el-Mallakh</a>. Inside were 1,224 pieces of wood, the longest 23 metres (75 ft) in length, the shortest 10 centimetres (0.33 ft). These were entrusted to a boat builder, Haj Ahmed Yusuf, who worked out how the pieces fit together. The entire process, including conservation and straightening of the warped wood, took fourteen years. The result is a cedar-wood boat 43.6 metres (143 ft) long, its timbers held together <a href="/wiki/Sewn_boat" title="Sewn boat">by ropes</a>, which was originally housed in the <a href="/wiki/Giza_Solar_boat_museum" title="Giza Solar boat museum">Giza Solar boat museum</a>, a special boat-shaped, air-conditioned museum beside the pyramid. It is now in the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Egyptian_Museum" title="Grand Egyptian Museum">Grand Egyptian Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>During construction of this museum in the 1980s, the second sealed boat pit was discovered. It was left unopened until 2011 when excavation began on the boat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pyramid_town">Pyramid town</span></h3>
<p>A notable construction flanking the Giza pyramid complex is a <a href="/wiki/Cyclopean_masonry" title="Cyclopean masonry">cyclopean</a> stone wall, the Wall of the Crow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[189]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_Lehner" title="Mark Lehner">Mark Lehner</a> discovered a worker's town outside of the wall, otherwise known as "The Lost City", dated by pottery styles, seal impressions and <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy" title="Stratigraphy">stratigraphy</a> to have been constructed and occupied sometime during the reigns of Khafre (2520–2494 BC) and Menkaure (2490–2472 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[191]</a></sup> In the early 21st century, Lehner and his team made several discoveries, including what appears to have been a thriving port, suggesting the town and associated living quarters, which consisted of barracks called "galleries", may not have been for the pyramid workers after all, but rather for the soldiers and sailors who utilized the port. In light of this new discovery, as to where then the pyramid workers may have lived, Lehner suggested the alternative possibility they may have camped on the ramps he believes were used to construct the pyramids, or possibly at nearby quarries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1970s, the Australian archaeologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Karl_Kromer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Karl Kromer (page does not exist)">Karl Kromer</a> excavated a mound in the South Field of the plateau. It was found to contain artefacts including mudbrick seals of Khufu, which he identified with an artisans' settlement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[193]</a></sup> Mudbrick buildings just south of Khufu's Valley Temple contained mud sealings of Khufu and have been suggested to be a settlement serving the cult of Khufu after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawassSenussi2008127–128_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawassSenussi2008127–128-196">[194]</a></sup> A worker's cemetery used at least between Khufu's reign and the end of the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Fifth Dynasty of Egypt">Fifth Dynasty</a> was discovered south of the Wall of the Crow by <a href="/wiki/Zahi_Hawass" title="Zahi Hawass">Hawass</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Looting">Looting</span></h2>
<p>Authors <a href="/wiki/Bob_Brier" title="Bob Brier">Bob Brier</a> and Hoyt Hobbs claim that "all the pyramids were robbed" by the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a>, when the construction of royal tombs in the <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_the_Kings" title="Valley of the Kings">Valley of the Kings</a> began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrierHobbs1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsdailylifeofancie00briepage164_164]_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrierHobbs1999[httpsarchiveorgdetailsdailylifeofancie00briepage164_164]-198">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECremin200796_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECremin200796-199">[197]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Tyldesley" title="Joyce Tyldesley">Joyce Tyldesley</a> states that the Great Pyramid itself "is known to have been opened and emptied by the Middle Kingdom", before the Arab <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a> entered the pyramid around 820 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETyldesley200738_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETyldesley200738-131">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I.E.S._Edwards" title="I.E.S. Edwards">I. E. S. Edwards</a> discusses <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>'s mention that the pyramid "a little way up one side has a stone that may be taken out, which being raised up there is a sloping passage to the foundations". Edwards suggested that the pyramid was entered by robbers after the end of the Old Kingdom and sealed and then reopened more than once until Strabo's door was added. He adds: "If this highly speculative surmise be correct, it is also necessary to assume either that the existence of the door was forgotten or that the entrance was again blocked with facing stones", in order to explain why al-Ma'mun could not find the entrance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards198699–100_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards198699–100-200">[198]</a></sup> Scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Gaston_Maspero" title="Gaston Maspero">Gaston Maspero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flinders_Petrie" title="Flinders Petrie">Flinders Petrie</a> have noted that evidence for a similar door has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Bent_Pyramid" title="Bent Pyramid">Bent Pyramid</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dashur" title="Dashur">Dashur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaspero1903181_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaspero1903181-201">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetrie189224–25,_167_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetrie189224–25,_167-202">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> visited Egypt in the 5th century BC and recounts a story that he was told concerning vaults under the pyramid built on an island where the body of Khufu lies. Edwards notes that the pyramid had "almost certainly been opened and its contents plundered long before the time of Herodotus" and that it might have been closed again during the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-sixth Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-sixth Dynasty of Egypt</a> when other monuments were restored. He suggests that the story told to Herodotus could have been the result of almost two centuries of telling and retelling by pyramid guides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdwards1986990–991_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdwards1986990–991-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1093669538">.mw-parser-output .portalbox{padding:0}.mw-parser-output .portalborder{border:solid #aaa 1px}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tleft{margin:0.5em 1em 0.5em 0}.mw-parser-output .portalbox.tright{margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul{display:table;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:175px;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .portalborder>ul{padding:0.1em;background:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:first-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .portalbox>ul>li>span:last-child{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle}</style><div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint plainlist portalbox portalborder tright" role="navigation">

<li id="cite_note-pe3617-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-pe3617_61-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-pe3617_61-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Naturalis Historia</a></i> 36.17.</span>

<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Itinerarium Egeriae</i> Y2 [= Peter the Deacon's citation]; ed. R. Weber, CCSL 175:100; <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=hvYQAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PT404#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">PL 173:1129D</a>; trans. Wilkinson 1999, 94.  This passage is not found in the sole surviving manuscript, which is only partially preserved, but appears in a later work by <a href="/wiki/Peter_the_Deacon" title="Peter the Deacon">Peter the Deacon</a> that uses Egeria as a source; see Wilkinson 1999, 4, 86.  Wilkinson is confident "this is the first text to mention what became the regular Christian explanation of the pyramids" (94 n. 4); cf. Osborne 1986, 115.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/Historia_monachorum_in_Aegypto" title="Historia monachorum in Aegypto">Historia monachorum in Aegypto</a></i> 18.3; ed. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=qjYsAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA79#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">Preuschen 1897, 79</a>; ed. Festugière 1971, 115; trans. Russell 1980, 102. There is also a Latin version by <a href="/wiki/Tyrannius_Rufinus" title="Tyrannius Rufinus">Rufinus</a>, which includes "additions and alterations appropriate to a man who had seen the places and people for himself and regarded the experience as the most treasured of his life" (Russell 1981, 6).  Rufinus seems a little less clear: "There is a tradition that these sites, which they call the storehouses (<i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">thesauros</i></span></i>) of Joseph, are where Joseph is said to have stored up the grain. Others say it is the Pyramids themselves in which it is thought that the grain was collected" (<a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=zRARAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=RA2-PA459#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">PL 21:440</a>; ed. Schulz-Flügel 1990, 350).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=FPs-AAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA73#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">Beazley 1897, 73</a>, says "writing in 376"; Nicolet 1991, 96, has "perhaps prior to A.D. 376"; and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brill%27s_New_Pauly" title="Brill's New Pauly">Brill's New Pauly</a></i> (Leiden, 2005), s.v. Iulius [= 6:1082] has "4th/5th cents."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Cosmographia</i> 45; ed. <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=dlgGAAAAQAAJ&amp;lpg=PA51&amp;ots=Ezqlu33fMS&amp;pg=PA51#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">Riese 1878, 51.2–4 (B)</a>; cf. Osborne 1986, 115. The quote appears only in version B of Riese's ed., a revision from late antiquity, and therefore may not derive from Julius.</span>
</li>
